reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 1D convolution operation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        output_padding (int, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to 0.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 1D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, length).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, length_out).
        """
        return self.conv1d_transpose(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 64
in_channels = 128
out_channels = 128
kernel_size = 3
# much larger signal length for heavier workload
length = 65536

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, length)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for transposed 1D convolution (ConvTranspose1d). The reference implementation uses `nn.ConvTranspose1d`. The parameters: `batch_size=64`, `in_channels=128`, `out_channels=128`, `kernel_size=3`, `stride=1`, `padding=0`, `output_padding=0`, `groups=1`, `bias=False` (by default). The length is `65536`. ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0 is effectively a standard transposed convolution that is equivalent to a normal convolution with flipped kernel? Actually for stride=1, ConvTranspose1d is equivalent to a standard convolution with the kernel rotated 180 degrees (but since it's learned, we can just treat it as a standard convolution with stride=1, padding=kernel_size-1 if we want the output to be larger? Wait, no. Let's recall: For `ConvTranspose1d`, output length = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel_size-1) + output_padding + 1. With stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1: L_out = (L_in - 1) * 1 - 0 + (kernel_size - 1) + 0 + 1 = L_in + kernel_size - 1. Wait, that means the output is longer than the input by `kernel_size - 1`. For kernel_size=3, L_out = L_in + 2 = 65538. But actually, `nn.ConvTranspose1d` is often used for upsampling. With stride=1 and padding=0, it's just a regular convolution but with the output size increased by kernel_size - 1. However, in PyTorch, `ConvTranspose1d` with stride=1 is actually equivalent to a standard `Conv1d` with appropriate padding to get the same output shape? Let's check. `nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=kernel_size-1)` gives output length = (L_in + 2*(kernel_size-1) - (kernel_size-1)) / 1 + 1 = L_in + kernel_size - 1. So yes, `ConvTranspose1d(..., stride=1, padding=0)` is equivalent to `Conv1d(..., stride=1, padding=kernel_size-1)` in terms of output shape, but the weight arrangement is transposed. Actually, the mathematical operation of transposed convolution with stride=1 is equivalent to a convolution with stride=1 but with the weight tensor shaped as `(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)` instead of `(out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size)`. Wait: In ConvTranspose1d, the weight shape is `(in_channels, out_channels/groups, kernel_size)`. In Conv1d, it's `(out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size)`. So if we have `groups=1`, ConvTranspose1d weight is `(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)`. The operation is: for each output position, sum over input channels and kernel positions. Let's write the formula for ConvTranspose1d (stride=1, padding=0): Output[b, c_out, t_out] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} Input[b, c_in, t_out - k] * Weight[c_in, c_out, k] where `t_out - k` must be within `[0, L_in-1]`. With padding=0, output length = L_in + K - 1. For t_out from 0 to L_in+K-2, the valid range of k is such that `t_out - k` is in [0, L_in-1], i.e., `max(0, t_out - (L_in-1)) <= k <= min(K-1, t_out)`. Alternatively, we can view it as a standard convolution of Input with Weight flipped along the kernel dimension and with padding = K-1 on both sides? Let's check. Standard Conv1d with weight W' of shape `(out_channels, in_channels, K)`, stride=1, padding=P: Output'[b, c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} Input[b, c_in, t + k - P] * W'[c_out, c_in, k] where `t + k - P` in [0, L_in-1]. If we set P = K-1, then: Output'[b, c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} Input[b, c_in, t + k - (K-1)] * W'[c_out, c_in, k]. Let j = K-1 - k, then k = K-1 - j: Output'[b, c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} sum_{j=0}^{K-1} Input[b, c_in, t - j] * W'[c_out, c_in, K-1-j]. Comparing to ConvTranspose1d: Output[b, c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} Input[b, c_in, t - k] * Weight[c_in, c_out, k]. So if we set W'[c_out, c_in, j] = Weight[c_in, c_out, K-1-j], then Output' = Output. So indeed, ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to Conv1d with stride=1, padding=K-1 and weight = Weight.transpose(0,1).flip(-1). But we don't need to match PyTorch's internal algorithm exactly; we just need to produce numerically equivalent outputs. So we can implement a direct CUDA kernel for the transposed convolution formula. Given the problem size: batch_size = 64 in_channels = 128 out_channels = 128 kernel_size = 3 length = 65536 So output length = 65536 + 3 - 1 = 65538. The total number of output elements = 64 * 128 * 65538 ≈ 537 million. This is a large problem. We need an efficient kernel. Let's think about the operation. It's essentially a matrix multiplication if we unfold the input. For each batch, we have: Input: (in_channels, L_in) Weight: (in_channels, out_channels, K) We can view it as: for each kernel position k, we shift the input by k and multiply by Weight[:, :, k]. Specifically: Output[:, :, t] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} Input[:, :, t-k] @ Weight[:, :, k] (where @ is matrix multiply over in_channels -> out_channels) More precisely, for a fixed batch b and kernel position k: For t from k to k + L_in - 1: Output[b, :, t] += Input[b, :, t-k] * Weight[:, :, k] (where * is a matrix multiplication: (out_channels) = (in_channels) @ (in_channels, out_channels)) Actually: Output[b, c_out, t] += sum_{c_in} Input[b, c_in, t-k] * Weight[c_in, c_out, k]. So for each k, we can compute a GEMM: Let X_k be Input[..., k:k+L_in] (but actually it's just Input shifted). The contribution for kernel index k is: X_k^T @ W_k, but we need to be careful with dimensions. Alternatively, we can reshape: Input: (batch_size, in_channels, L_in) Weight: (in_channels, out_channels, K) For each batch b and kernel k: - We take Input[b, :, :] of shape (in_channels, L_in) - Weight[:, :, k] of shape (in_channels, out_channels) - Compute GEMM: (out_channels, L_in) = (out_channels, in_channels) @ (in_channels, L_in)? Alternatively, we can use `torch.matmul`: - x reshaped to (B * L_in, IC) - w_k reshaped to (IC, OC) - gemm = (B * L_in, IC) @ (IC, OC) = (B * L_in, OC) - reshape to (B, L_in, OC) - transpose to (B, OC, L_in) - accumulate to output with offset. Actually, if we do 3 separate GEMMs and accumulate, we need to either: - Allocate a temporary for each gemm and add, or - Do the GEMM and add in-place to output using `beta=1` in GEMM (i.e., C = alpha * A @ B + beta * C). PyTorch's `addmm` or `baddbmm` supports this, but with batching it's tricky. For the first GEMM (k=0), we can initialize output[:, :, 0:L_in] = gemm0. For k=1, we compute gemm1 and add to output[:, :, 1:L_in+1]. For k=2, compute gemm2 and add to output[:, :, 2:L_in+2]. In PyTorch: ```python out = torch.zeros(B, OC, L_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) for k in range(K): wk = self.weight[:, :, k] # (IC, OC) gemm = torch.matmul(x.transpose(1,2), wk) # (B, L_in, OC) out[:, :, k:k+L_in] += gemm.transpose(1,2) ``` But `x.transpose(1,2)` is (B, L_in, IC). `matmul` of (B, L_in, IC) @ (IC, OC) -> (B, L_in, OC). This is batched matrix multiply where the batch dimension is B. cuBLAS handles this as a strided batched GEMM. The effective M = L_in = 65536, N = 128, K = 128, batch = 64. This is also very efficient. Wait, is `(B, L_in, IC) @ (IC, OC)` valid? In PyTorch, for 3D tensor @ 2D tensor, it treats the 2D tensor as batch-shared. Yes: `(B, M, K) @ (K, N) -> (B, M, N)`. This is supported and calls a batched GEMM. So the code is: ```python x_perm = x.transpose(1, 2) # (B, L_in, IC) out = torch.zeros(B, OC, L_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) for k in range(K): wk = self.weight[:, :, k] # (IC, OC) contrib = torch.matmul(x_perm, wk) # (B, L_in, OC) out[:, :, k:k+L_in] += contrib.transpose(1, 2) ``` This should be quite fast. Let's try writing a custom kernel. The kernel design: We process the output tensor in tiles. Grid: - blockIdx.x: time tile index - blockIdx.y: output channel tile index - blockIdx.z: batch index Block: - Threads: maybe 256 threads per block. - Each thread computes a segment of output channels and time. If we have threads in a warp accessing consecutive `t`, then the addresses are consecutive. Good. So for loading input shared memory, we can have each warp load a slice of (ic_local, t_local). For example, for input_shared[IC_TILE][T+K-1], we want to fill it such that `input_shared[ic_local][t_local]` holds `input[b, ic_base + ic_local, t_base + t_local - (K-1)]` if in bounds, else 0. We can assign the 256 threads to iterate over the 64*130 = 8320 elements. Each thread loads ceil(8320/256) = 33 elements. But to ensure coalescing, consecutive threads should load consecutive elements in the `t` dimension for the same `ic`. So we can organize loading as: ``` int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; for (int ic_local = warp_id; ic_local < IC_TILE; ic_local += num_warps) { for (int t_local = lane_id; t_local < T + K - 1; t_local += 32) { int t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1); float val = 0; if (t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_local) * L_in + t_global)]; } input_shared[ic_local][t_local] = val; } } ``` This ensures each warp loads one ic_local, and threads in warp load consecutive t_local. Since the global memory for fixed ic and varying t is contiguous, this is fully coalesced. But note: if `num_warps = 8` (256 threads / 32), and `IC_TILE = 64`, then each warp handles 8 ic_locals (ic_local = warp_id, warp_id+8, warp_id+16, ...). That's okay. The memory accesses for different ic_locals are separated by `L_in` floats, which is large, so they are not coalesced across ic_locals. But within a warp, all threads access the same ic_local and consecutive t, so they are coalesced. Similarly for weight loading: Weight is stored as (IC, OC, K) with stride (OC*K, K, 1). So for fixed ic and k, oc is contiguous. We need weight_shared[IC_TILE][OC_TILE][K]. We can load by having warps iterate over ic_local, and lanes iterate over oc_local and k. For a given ic_local and k, weight[ic_base+ic_local, oc_base+oc_local, k] is contiguous in oc_local. So we can have a warp load for a fixed ic_local and k, with lanes loading consecutive oc_local. ``` for (int ic_local = warp_id; ic_local < IC_TILE; ic_local += num_warps) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { for (int oc_local = lane_id; oc_local < OC_TILE; oc_local += 32) { weight_shared[ic_local][oc_local][k] = weight[((ic_base + ic_local) * (OC/groups) + (oc_base + oc_local)) * K + k]; } } } ``` But wait, `weight_shared` is a 3D array. In CUDA, shared memory is linear. We can define it as a 1D array and index manually. Layout for input_shared: we can use `float input_shared[IC_TILE * (T + K - 1)];` and index as `ic_local * (T+K-1) + t_local`. This is fine. Layout for weight_shared: `float weight_shared[IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K];` index as `(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K + k`. The loading loops: For input: ``` for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IC_TILE * (T + K - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int ic_local = idx / (T + K - 1); int t_local = idx % (T + K - 1); int t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_local) * L_in + t_global)]; } input_shared[idx] = val; } ``` But with blockDim.x = 256, threads load `idx = threadIdx.x, threadIdx.x+256, ...`. For idx=0..255, ic_local = 0, t_local = idx. These threads all have ic_local=0, t_local varying. Addresses are contiguous. For idx=256, ic_local=1, t_local=0. Threads 0 and 256 are in different warps (warp 0 and warp 8). Within a warp, addresses are contiguous. So coalescing is good! Because each warp handles a contiguous range of idx. For idx=0..31, ic_local=0, t_local=0..31 -> contiguous addresses. For idx=32..63, ic_local=0, t_local=32..63 -> contiguous addresses. So each warp is fully coalesced. Good. Similarly for weight: ``` for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K; idx += blockDim.x) { int ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K); int rem = idx % (OC_TILE * K); int oc_local = rem / K; int k = rem % K; // load weight[ic_base+ic_local, oc_base+oc_local, k] } ``` Weight storage: `weight[((ic_base + ic_local) * (OC/groups) + (oc_base + oc_local)) * K + k]`. Wait, `weight` is a 1D pointer. The original shape is `(in_channels, out_channels//groups, kernel_size)`. The stride is `( (out_channels//groups) * kernel_size, kernel_size, 1 )`. So offset = `((ic_base + ic_local) * (OC/groups) + (oc_base + oc_local)) * K + k`. For idx=0..255, ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K). If OC_TILE=16, K=3, then OC_TILE*K=48. So idx=0..47 => ic_local=0, rem=0..47. oc_local = rem/3 (0..15), k=rem%3. For consecutive idx, oc_local is mostly consecutive (3 values per oc). So memory accesses are mostly contiguous. Specifically, idx=0 loads weight[ic=0,oc=0,k=0], idx=1 loads [0,0,1], idx=2 loads [0,0,2], idx=3 loads [0,1,0], etc. Since for fixed ic and varying oc, weight is contiguous in oc and k. So addresses are contiguous. Great, fully coalesced. So the flat loading is fine and simple. After loading shared memory, we need to synchronize. Then each thread computes its assigned outputs. The loops: ``` for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = threadIdx.y * 2 + j; int oc = oc_base + oc_local; if (oc >= OC) break; // in case OC not divisible by OC_TILE for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; int t = t_base + t_local; if (t >= L_out) break; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float in_val = input_shared[ic_local * (T + K - 1) + (t_local + (K - 1) - k)]; float w_val = weight_shared[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } // accumulate to global output int out_idx = ((b * OC + oc) * L_out + t); // For first ic_base, just write; for subsequent, add. if (ic_base == 0) { output[out_idx] = sum; } else { output[out_idx] += sum; } } } ``` Wait, we need to handle the accumulation over ic_base correctly. We can initialize output for the first tile and add for subsequent tiles. But there is a race condition if multiple blocks write to the same output. However, each block handles a unique (b, oc_base, t_base) tile, so no two blocks write to the same output element. So `output[out_idx] = (ic_base == 0 ? sum : output[out_idx] + sum);` is safe if we know that the block processes the entire tile. But reading and writing global memory in a loop over ic_local might be slow. Better to accumulate in a register, and after the IC loop, write once. So: ``` float accum[2][4] = {0}; for (int ic_iter = 0; ic_iter < IC_TILE; ++ic_iter) { // load input for this ic_iter? Actually input is in shared for all ic_local. // we loop ic_local inside. } ``` Wait, we process IC in tiles: outer loop over `ic_base`. For each `ic_base`, we load input and weight for `ic` in `[ic_base, ic_base+IC_TILE)`. Then inside the block, each thread loops over all `ic_local` in `0..IC_TILE-1`. So the accumulation for a given output is done across the entire IC_TILE in one go. Then after the `ic_local` loops, we write to output. But the outer `ic_base` loop is inside the block? Actually, the block loops over `ic_base` from 0 to `IC` step `IC_TILE`. For each iteration, it loads shared memory and computes partial sums, accumulating in registers. After all `ic_base` iterations, it writes the final result to global memory. This is much better! So: Pseudo-code for the block: ``` // Registers for accumulation float acc[2][4] = {0}; for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input_shared and weight_shared __syncthreads(); // load code here... __syncthreads(); // Compute for j in 0..1: int oc_local = threadIdx.y * 2 + j; for i in 0..3: int t_local = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; float s = 0; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float in_val = input_shared[ic_local * (T + K - 1) + (t_local + (K - 1) - k)]; float w_val = weight_shared[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K + k]; s += in_val * w_val; } } acc[j][i] += s; } } } // Write back for j in 0..1: int oc_local = threadIdx.y * 2 + j; int oc = oc_base + oc_local; if (oc >= OC) continue; for i in 0..3: int t_local = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; int t = t_base + t_local; if (t >= L_out) continue; output[((b * OC + oc) * L_out + t)] = acc[j][i]; ``` But wait: `oc_base` is the block's output channel tile (e.g., 0, 16, 32, ...). And `ic_base` loops over input channels. This is correct. Now, we need to handle `groups > 1` in general. But for the fast path, we will assume groups==1. Also, we need to handle `stride`, `padding`, `output_padding`, `bias`. The constructor allows them. Since the test uses defaults, we can either: 1. Now, writing the CUDA code. First, we need to write the C++ source for `load_inline`. The kernel signature: ``` void conv_transpose1d_fast( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int IC, int OC, int K, int L_in, int L_out ) ``` Inside the kernel: ``` constexpr int T = 128; constexpr int OC_TILE = 16; constexpr int IC_TILE = 64; // Block dims: (32, 8) -> 256 threads int b = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int oc_base = oc_block * OC_TILE; int t_base = t_block * T; int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 // each thread handles 4 time, 2 oc int t_local_start = tx * 4; int oc_local_start = ty * 2; __shared__ float s_input[IC_TILE * (T + K - 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K]; float acc[2][4] = {0.0f}; for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input into shared int input_total = IC_TILE * (T + K - 1); for (int idx = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; idx < input_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (T + K - 1); int t_local = idx % (T + K - 1); int t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_local) * L_in + t_global)]; } s_input[idx] = val; } // Load weight into shared int weight_total = IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K; for (int idx = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; idx < weight_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K); int rem = idx % (OC_TILE * K); int oc_local = rem / K; int k = rem % K; float val = 0.0f; int oc_global = oc_base + oc_local; if (ic_base + ic_local < IC && oc_global < OC) { val = weight[((ic_base + ic_local) * OC + oc_global) * K + k]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial accumulation #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; if (oc_base + oc_local >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; if (t_base + t_local >= L_out) continue; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { // prefetch? maybe not needed #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float in_val = s_input[ic_local * (T + K - 1) + (t_local + (K - 1) - k)]; float w_val = s_weight[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } acc[j][i] += sum; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; int oc = oc_base + oc_local; if (oc >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; int t = t_base + t_local; if (t >= L_out) continue; float val = acc[j][i]; if (bias != nullptr) { val += bias[oc]; } output[((b * OC + oc) * L_out + t)] = val; } } ``` Wait, there's a bug: `L_out = L_in + K - 1` for stride=1, padding=0. But in general, we should pass L_out. Also, the weight indexing: weight shape is (IC, OC/groups, K). For groups=1, it's (IC, OC, K). So offset = `((ic * OC) + oc) * K + k`. That's correct. But what if IC is not a multiple of IC_TILE? The loop `for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE)` will have a last iteration where `ic_base + ic_local >= IC` for some `ic_local`. We guard the weight load with `if (ic_base + ic_local < IC && oc_global < OC)`. For input load, we also need `ic_base + ic_local < IC`: ``` if (ic_base + ic_local < IC && t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in) { ... } ``` If `ic_base + ic_local >= IC`, the input is out of bounds. We set to 0. Similarly for weight load, if `ic_base + ic_local >= IC` or `oc_global >= OC`, weight=0. In the computation loop, for `ic_local` from 0 to `IC_TILE-1`, if `ic_base + ic_local >= IC`, then `s_input` and `s_weight` would be 0, so sum is unaffected. Good. But `IC_TILE` is a compile-time constant, and we unroll the inner loop. If `IC` is not a multiple of `IC_TILE`, the last iteration will have some `s_input` and `s_weight` zeroed. That's fine. Let's implement the kernel described. But we need to be careful with compile options. H100 is compute capability 9.0. We should compile with `-arch=sm_90` or let PyTorch figure it out. Let's draft the code. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define T 128 #define OC_TILE 16 #define IC_TILE 64 #define K_MAX 3 __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int L_in, int IC, int OC, int K, int L_out ) { int b = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int oc_base = oc_block * OC_TILE; int t_base = t_block * T; if (b >= B) return; if (oc_base >= OC) return; if (t_base >= L_out) return; int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 // each thread handles 4 t and 2 oc int t_local_start = tx * 4; int oc_local_start = ty * 2; __shared__ float s_input[IC_TILE * (T + K_MAX - 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_MAX]; float acc[2][4]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { acc[j][i] = 0.0f; } } for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // Load input int input_total = IC_TILE * (T + K_MAX - 1); for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < input_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (T + K_MAX - 1); int t_local = idx % (T + K_MAX - 1); int t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in && ic_base + ic_local < IC) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_local) * L_in + t_global)]; } s_input[idx] = val; } // Load weight int weight_total = IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_MAX; for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < weight_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K_MAX); int rem = idx % (OC_TILE * K_MAX); int oc_local = rem / K_MAX; int k = rem % K_MAX; float val = 0.0f; int oc_global = oc_base + oc_local; if (ic_base + ic_local < IC && oc_global < OC && k < K) { val = weight[((ic_base + ic_local) * OC + oc_global) * K + k]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; if (oc_base + oc_local >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; if (t_base + t_local >= L_out) continue; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { // If ic_base + ic_local >= IC, s_input and s_weight are zero, so skip for speed? // But unrolling is more important, let it multiply zero. #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K_MAX; ++k) { if (k >= K) break; // K_MAX is 3, K could be 1,2,3 float in_val = s_input[ic_local * (T + K_MAX - 1) + (t_local + (K_MAX - 1) - k)]; float w_val = s_weight[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K_MAX + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } acc[j][i] += sum; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write out #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; int oc = oc_base + oc_local; if (oc >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; int t = t_base + t_local; if (t >= L_out) continue; float val = acc[j][i]; if (bias != nullptr) { val += bias[oc]; } output[((b * OC + oc) * L_out + t)] = val; } } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int output_padding, int groups ) { // Fast path: stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, groups=1 // Otherwise fall back to PyTorch functional if (stride != 1 || padding != 0 || output_padding != 0 || groups != 1) { // Fall back if (bias.has_value()) { return torch::nn::functional::conv_transpose1d( input, weight, bias.value(), torch::nn::functional::ConvTranspose1dFuncOptions() .stride(stride).padding(padding).output_padding(output_padding).groups(groups) ); } else { return torch::nn::functional::conv_transpose1d( input, weight, torch::nn::functional::ConvTranspose1dFuncOptions() .stride(stride).padding(padding).output_padding(output_padding).groups(groups) ); } } int B = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int L_in = input.size(2); int OC = weight.size(1) * groups; // weight is (IC, OC/groups, K) int K = weight.size(2); // For our kernel, K must be <= K_MAX (3) if (K > K_MAX) { if (bias.has_value()) { return torch::nn::functional::conv_transpose1d( input, weight, bias.value(), torch::nn::functional::ConvTranspose1dFuncOptions() .stride(stride).padding(padding).output_padding(output_padding).groups(groups) ); } else { return torch::nn::functional::conv_transpose1d( input, weight, torch::nn::functional::ConvTranspose1dFuncOptions() .stride(stride).padding(padding).output_padding(output_padding).groups(groups) ); } } int L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + K - 1 + output_padding + 1; torch::Tensor output = torch::empty({B, OC, L_out}, input.options()); dim3 threads(32, 8); dim3 blocks( (L_out + T - 1) / T, (OC + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE, B ); conv_transpose1d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), B, L_in, IC, OC, K, L_out ); return output; } ''' # Then in ModelNew.forward: # out = conv_transpose1d_cuda(x, self.weight, self.bias, self.stride, self.padding, self.output_padding, self.groups) ``` Wait, PyTorch C++ API for `torch::nn::functional::conv_transpose1d` might not be directly available or might require including headers. It's easier to call Python `torch.nn.functional.conv_transpose1d` from the Python side. So in the C++ code, we only need the kernel for the fast path. For fallback, we can handle it in Python. Yes! That's simpler. In `forward`, we can check if params are default. If not, call `F.conv_transpose1d(x, self.weight, self.bias, self.stride, self.padding, self.output_padding, self.groups)`. If yes, call the CUDA function. This avoids needing C++ fallback. Let's write the code accordingly. One more thing: the kernel uses `K_MAX=3`. The test uses `kernel_size=3`. What if kernel_size is 1 or 2? Our constructor may be called with other sizes, but the test uses 3. We can handle `K=1` and `K=2` by padding with zeros in shared memory. The kernel has `if (k >= K) continue;` but we unroll over `K_MAX`. Wait, we have `#pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K_MAX; ++k) { if (k >= K) break; ... }`. Since K_MAX=3, this is fine. For K=1, the inner loop will break after k=0. For K=2, after k=1. But the `s_input` loading uses `K - 1` for padding. Wait, `t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1)`. This depends on K. Also `s_input` size is `T + K_MAX - 1 = T + 2`. For K=1, we only need input at `t_base + t_local`, but our indexing `t_local + (K_MAX - 1) - k` with K_MAX=3 gives `t_local + 2` for k=0, `t_local + 1` for k=1, `t_local` for k=2. But for K=1, we only use k=0. Then `in_val = s_input[ic_local * (T+2) + (t_local + 2)]`. This is wrong because the input loaded at `t_local` corresponds to `t_global = t_base + t_local - (K-1) = t_base + t_local`. But we indexed it as `s_input[... + t_local + (K_MAX-1)]` which is `t_local + 2`. This is a mismatch. So `K_MAX` cannot be hardcoded to 3 for general K. We need the shared memory size and indexing to depend on K. But we can make K_MAX a template parameter or just a compile-time constant if we only care about K=3. Since the test uses K=3, and we have a fallback for other K, we can just assert K <= K_MAX and adjust indexing for K. Wait, if we load input into `s_input` with size `T + K - 1` (actual K, not K_MAX), then indexing uses `t_local + (K - 1) - k`. For K=3, size is T+2. For K=1, size is T, and indexing is `t_local - k = t_local`. That works. But using dynamic K for shared memory size means we cannot have static shared memory arrays with sizes depending on K (a runtime variable) in C++? Actually, we can: `extern __shared__ float sdata[];` and compute offsets. Or we can over-allocate: `__shared__ float s_input[IC_TILE * (T + K_MAX - 1)];` and then when K < K_MAX, we load fewer elements and adjust indexing. Let's see: we load input for t_global from `t_base - (K-1)` to `t_base + T - 1`. The number of elements is `T + K - 1`. We store them in `s_input[ic_local * (T + K_MAX - 1) + t_local]` where `t_local` goes from 0 to `T+K-2`. For indexing, we need `s_input[ic_local * (T + K_MAX - 1) + (t_local + (K - 1) - k)]`. But the pitch is `(T + K_MAX - 1)`, while actual data length per ic_local is `(T + K - 1)`. The extra space is unused. That works! For K=1: - Load: `t_local` in 0..T-1. `t_global = t_base + t_local`. Stored at `ic_local * (T+2) + t_local`. - Compute: for k=0, index = `t_local + (1-1) - 0 = t_local`. So `s_input[ic_local*(T+2) + t_local]`. That's correct because that's where we stored it. For K=2: - Load: `t_local` in 0..T. `t_global = t_base + t_local - 1`. Stored at `ic_local*(T+2) + t_local`. - Compute: k=0: index = `t_local + 1 - 0 = t_local + 1`. k=1: index = `t_local + 1 - 1 = t_local`. Correct. For K=3: - Load: `t_local` in 0..T+1. `t_global = t_base + t_local - 2`. Stored at `ic_local*(T+2) + t_local`. - Compute: k=0: `t_local+2`. k=1: `t_local+1`. k=2: `t_local`. Correct. So using a pitch of `T + K_MAX - 1` works for any `K <= K_MAX`. Similarly for weight: we allocate `IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_MAX`. For actual K < K_MAX, we load weight up to K and leave the rest zero. In compute loop, we break when `k >= K`. The indexing `ic_local*OC_TILE*K_MAX + oc_local*K_MAX + k` is fine. Wait, weight layout in shared memory: we used `s_weight[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K_MAX + k]`. For K=1, we load at k=0. The rest (k=1,2) are zero. In compute, we loop k=0 only. Good. So we can keep K_MAX=3 and support K=1,2,3. Now, what about `K > K_MAX`? The test uses K=3. For K>3, we can fall back to PyTorch. Let's write the full Python file. Wait, we need to be careful: `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` might take some time to compile. That's okay. Also, we need to compute output shape in Python for the fast path: `L_out = (x.size(-1) - 1) * self.stride - 2 * self.padding + self.dilation * (self.kernel_size - 1) + self.output_padding + 1` But in the fast path, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1. So `L_out = x.size(-1) + self.kernel_size - 1`. Let's write the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> constexpr int T = 128; constexpr int OC_TILE = 16; constexpr int IC_TILE = 64; constexpr int K_MAX = 3; __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int L_in, int IC, int OC, int K, int L_out ) { int b = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int oc_base = oc_block * OC_TILE; int t_base = t_block * T; if (b >= B) return; if (oc_base >= OC) return; if (t_base >= L_out) return; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int t_local_start = tx * 4; int oc_local_start = ty * 2; __shared__ float s_input[IC_TILE * (T + K_MAX - 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_MAX]; float acc[2][4]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { acc[j][i] = 0.0f; } } for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { int input_total = IC_TILE * (T + K_MAX - 1); for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < input_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (T + K_MAX - 1); int t_local = idx % (T + K_MAX - 1); int t_global = t_base + t_local - (K - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (t_global >= 0 && t_global < L_in && ic_base + ic_local < IC) { val = input[((b * IC + ic_base + ic_local) * L_in + t_global)]; } s_input[idx] = val; } int weight_total = IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K_MAX; for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < weight_total; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K_MAX); int rem = idx % (OC_TILE * K_MAX); int oc_local = rem / K_MAX; int k = rem % K_MAX; float val = 0.0f; int oc_global = oc_base + oc_local; if (ic_base + ic_local < IC && oc_global < OC && k < K) { val = weight[((ic_base + ic_local) * OC + oc_global) * K + k]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; if (oc_base + oc_local >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; if (t_base + t_local >= L_out) continue; float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K_MAX; ++k) { if (k >= K) break; float in_val = s_input[ic_local * (T + K_MAX - 1) + (t_local + (K_MAX - 1) - k)]; float w_val = s_weight[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K_MAX + k]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } acc[j][i] += sum; } } __syncthreads(); } #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 2; ++j) { int oc_local = oc_local_start + j; int oc = oc_base + oc_local; if (oc >= OC) continue; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int t_local = t_local_start + i; int t = t_base + t_local; if (t >= L_out) continue; float val = acc[j][i]; if (bias != nullptr) { val += bias[oc]; } output[((b * OC + oc) * L_out + t)] = val; } } } torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_fast( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int output_padding, int groups ) { int B = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int L_in = input.size(2); int OC = weight.size(1) * groups; int K = weight.size(2); int L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + (K - 1) + output_padding + 1; torch::Tensor output = torch::empty({B, OC, L_out}, input.options()); dim3 threads(32, 8); dim3 blocks( (L_out + T - 1) / T, (OC + OC_TILE - 1) / OC_TILE, B ); conv_transpose1d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), B, L_in, IC, OC, K, L_out ); return output; } ''' conv_transpose_cuda = load_inline( name="conv_transpose1d_fast", cpp_sources="", # we only have cuda source? load_inline requires cpp_sources or cuda_sources. # Actually, we put the C++/CUDA code in cpp_sources or cuda_sources. cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["conv_transpose1d_fast"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride self.padding = padding self.output_padding = output_padding self.groups = groups self.dilation = 1 # default self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty( in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size )) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) # Initialize nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) if fan_in != 0: bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: if (self.stride == 1 and self.padding == 0 and self.output_padding == 0 and self.groups == 1 and x.dtype == torch.float32 and self.kernel_size <= 3): return conv_transpose_cuda.conv_transpose1d_fast( x, self.weight, self.bias, self.stride, self.padding, self.output_padding, self.groups ) else: return F.conv_transpose1d( x, self.weight, self.bias, stride=self.stride, padding=self.padding, output_padding=self.output_padding, groups=self.groups, dilation=self.dilation ) ``` Wait, `load_inline` with `cuda_sources` may require `cpp_sources` to declare the function? The kernel uses too much shared memory. Let me reduce the shared memory usage. With IC_TILE=128, T=128, K_MAX=3: - s_input: 128 * (128 + 2) * 4 bytes = about 65536 bytes - s_weight: 128 * 16 * 3 * 4 bytes = 24576 bytes Total = ~90 KB, which should fit in 227 KB... Maybe it's the default limit and we need to add an option or maybe for dynamic shared it defaults to 48KB. For sm_80+ the limit is 164KB or 227KB per block but statically allocated might have a lower limit in ptxas... Actually for sm_90, the shared memory limit per block is 227KB but the compile-time limit for static shared memory in a single kernel is 48KB unless you set shared memory carveout or use dynamic allocation. Actually, CUDA limits static shared memory to 48KB per block for older architectures, but for sm_80+ it's 100KB or more? The error says 0xc000 max which is 49152 (48 KB). This is likely a ptxas compile-time limitation for static shared memory in a kernel. We need to either use dynamic shared memory with `extern __shared__` or reduce the sizes to fit in 48KB. Let me reduce T to 64 and OC_TILE to 8: - s_input: 128 * (64 + 2) * 4 = 128 * 66 * 4 = 33792 bytes - s_weight: 128 * 8 * 3 * 4 = 12288 bytes Total = 46080 bytes < 49152. Good. But we also need to make sure occupancy is decent. Let me also consider using an implicit gemm / wmma approach or cuBLAS? Actually, ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to conv1d with stride=1 after flipping/reversing the kernel appropriately? Wait, conv_transpose1d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0 is actually numerically equivalent to conv1d with stride=1, padding=kernel_size-1, but with the weight applied differently. Actually, the simplest way: for stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, conv_transpose1d is mathematically identical to conv1d with the same weight but with appropriate padding. Wait, is that right? For ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0: Output size L_out = (L_in - 1)*1 - 2*0 + (K-1) + 0 + 1 = L_in + K - 1 - 1 + 1 = L_in + K - 1? Wait, the formula is: L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K-1) + output_padding + 1. With dilation=1, stride=1, padding=0: L_out = (L_in-1) - 0 + (K-1) + 0 + 1 = L_in + K - 1. For conv1d with stride=1, padding = K-1: L_out = L_in + 2*(K-1) - (K-1) = L_in + K - 1. So the output sizes match. But are the computations the same? Conv1d: out[b, oc, t] = sum_ic sum_k in[b, ic, t + k] * w[oc, ic, k] (with appropriate padding handling, and reversed kernel indexing depending on implementation). The columns of the conv matrix are shifted versions of the kernel. Let me try the approach of writing a direct kernel but being careful about shared memory. I'll write a kernel that does the following: - Tile the output in time dimension with T=64 and in OC dimension with OC_TILE=8. - Loop over IC in tiles of IC_TILE=64. - For each IC tile, load the required input segment (64 time positions + K-1 overlap) into shared memory. - Load the weight tile (IC_TILE x OC_TILE x K) into shared memory. - Compute the partial sums. Let me think about the kernel structure. ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose1d_k3s1p0_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int L, int IC, int OC) { // input: [B, IC, L] // weight: [IC, OC, 3] // output: [B, OC, L+2] int b = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y; int t_block = blockIdx.x; int oc0 = oc_block * OC_TILE; int t0 = t_block * T; if (b >= B || oc0 >= OC || t0 >= L+2) return; int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 // Each thread handles: // oc_local = ty, so oc = oc0 + ty // t positions: tx*2 and tx*2+1 int t1 = t0 + tx*2; int t2 = t1 + 1; float acc1 = 0.0f, acc2 = 0.0f; __shared__ float s_in[IC_TILE * (T + 2)]; __shared__ float s_w[IC_TILE * OC_TILE * 3]; // iterate over IC tiles for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_TILE) { // cooperatively load input tile // input needed for output positions [t0, t0+T-1] // For conv_transpose1d with K=3, output at t uses input at t, t-1, t-2 // Wait, we need the indexing formula. // conv_transpose1d formula for stride=1, padding=0: // out[b, oc, t] = sum_ic sum_k=0..K-1 weight[ic, oc, k] * input[b, ic, t - k] // (assuming input is zero outside [0, L-1]) // So for t in [t0, t0+T-1], we need input at [t0-K+1, t0+T-1] int in_t0 = t0 - (K - 1); // first input index we might need int in_len = T + K - 1; // Load input tile into shared memory for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < IC_TILE * in_len; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / in_len; int t_local = idx % in_len; int ic = ic_base + ic_local; int t_global = in_t0 + t_local; float val = 0.0f; if (ic < IC && t_global >= 0 && t_global < L) { val = input[((b * IC + ic) * L + t_global)]; } s_in[idx] = val; } // Load weight tile for (int idx = ty * blockDim.x + tx; idx < IC_TILE * OC_TILE * K; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int ic_local = idx / (OC_TILE * K); int rem = idx % (OC_TILE * K); int oc_local = rem / K; int k = rem % K; int ic = ic_base + ic_local; int oc = oc0 + oc_local; float val = 0.0f; if (ic < IC && oc < OC) { val = weight[((ic * OC + oc) * K + k)]; } s_w[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes for its assigned (oc=oc0+ty, t=t1 and t=t2) int oc_local = ty; int oc = oc0 + oc_local; if (oc < OC) { // For t1: if (t1 < L + 2) { float sum1 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { // input indices for t1: t1, t1-1, t1-2 // in shared memory, these are stored at: // For t_global = in_t0 + t_local, we have s_in[ic_local * in_len + t_local] // We need t_global = t1, t1-1, t1-2 // t_local = t_global - in_t0 = t1 - (t0 - 2) = t1 - t0 + 2 // Since t1 = t0 + tx*2, t_local = tx*2 + 2 // Similarly for k: we need input at t1 - k // t_local = (t1 - k) - in_t0 = tx*2 + 2 - k #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 3; ++k) { float in_val = s_in[ic_local * in_len + (tx*2 + 2 - k)]; float w_val = s_w[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * 3 + k]; sum1 += in_val * w_val; } } acc1 += sum1; } // For t2: if (t2 < L + 2) { float sum2 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_TILE; ++ic_local) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 3; ++k) { float in_val = s_in[ic_local * in_len + (tx*2 + 3 - k)]; float w_val = s_w[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * 3 + k]; sum2 += in_val * w_val; } } acc2 += sum2; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write results int oc = oc0 + ty; if (oc < OC) { if (t1 < L + 2) { output[((b * OC + oc) * (L+2) + t1)] = acc1; } if (t2 < L + 2) { output[((b * OC + oc) * (L+2) + t2)] = acc2; } } } ``` Wait, I need to verify the indexing formula. But I can hint it with `#pragma unroll` on the k loop. `#pragma unroll` without a number tells the compiler to fully unroll if possible. But if K is not a compile-time constant, it might not unroll. Let's check: in CUDA, `#pragma unroll` on a loop with a non-constant trip count does not unroll it. It needs a constant or a `#pragma unroll N`. Actually, the compiler can sometimes unroll if it can determine bounds, but with a function parameter, it's variable per launch. To guarantee unrolling, I should use a template for K or a switch with explicitly unrolled versions. But we can also just use a `switch (K)` at C++ level to call templated kernels. That's the standard approach. Let me write the templated kernel. ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel(...) { // ... #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { ... } } ``` Then in the launcher: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_fast(...) { // ... switch (K) { case 1: conv_transpose1d_kernel<1><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 2: conv_transpose1d_kernel<2><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 3: conv_transpose1d_kernel<3><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 4: conv_transpose1d_kernel<4><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 5: conv_transpose1d_kernel<5><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 6: conv_transpose1d_kernel<6><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; case 7: conv_transpose1d_kernel<7><<<blocks, threads>>>(...); break; default: TORCH_CHECK(false, "Unsupported kernel size"); } } ``` This compiles separate kernels for K=1..7. The binary size increases but it's small. And for K=3, the inner loop is fully unrolled. OK, let's finalize the code. For the custom kernel, I need to compute it exactly as PyTorch does: `L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1`. For our fast path, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0. So L_out = L_in + K - 1. But to be safe, I'll compute it using the same formula. Now, about bias: after the kernel, I need to add bias if present. In PyTorch, bias is added per channel: `out += bias.view(1, -1, 1)`. I can do this with a simple element-wise kernel or just let PyTorch handle it: `out = out + self.conv1d_transpose.bias.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(-1)`. This is efficient enough. The correctness check failed. The issue is likely that my manual conv transpose implementation doesn't match PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d exactly. Let me think about the data layout and weight ordering. In PyTorch ConvTranspose1d: - `weight` has shape `(in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size)` - The actual operation is essentially a cross-correlation from the input to output, but with stride-1 default it's the dilation of input with the flipped kernel. For ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0: - Output length = L + K - 1 - The operation is: for each output position t, it's a sum over input positions k: input[..., k] * weight[..., t - k] Wait, maybe my indexing is wrong. Let me reconsider the convolution operation. For ConvTranspose1d (also called deconvolution): - For `stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1`: - output[n, c_out, t_out] = sum_{c_in} sum_{k} input[n, c_in, t_out - k + pad] * weight[c_in, c_out, k] Actually, the PyTorch documentation says for ConvTranspose1d: `out[n, c_out, t] = sum_k weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[n, c_in, stride * t + dilation * k - padding]` Wait, no. Let me re-read carefully. For ConvTranspose1d, the weight shape is `(in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size)` when `bias=False`. The operation is actually: ``` out[N, C_out, L_out] = conv_transpose1d(in, weight, bias, stride, padding, output_padding, groups, dilation) ``` For stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1: ``` out[n, g*C_out/g + c_out, t_out] = sum_{c_in, k} weight[g*C_in/g + c_in, c_out, k] * input[n, g*C_in/g + c_in, t_out - k + padding] ``` Wait: for ConvTranspose1d with padding=0, stride=1: - The relation is that `conv_transpose1d(conv1d(x, w, stride=1, padding=0), w, stride=1, padding=0) ≈ x` (roughly) For a regular conv1d with stride=1, padding=0: `out[t] = sum_k w[k] * x[t + k]` For the transpose with same settings: `out[t] = sum_k w[k] * x[t - k]` But PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d with weight shape `(in, out, k)` maps: `out[n, c_out, t] = sum_{c_in} sum_k weight[c_in, c_out, k] * input[n, c_in, t - k]` when stride=1, padding=0. Let me verify. Actually, the PyTorch manual says for ConvTranspose1d: - `out[n, c_out, t] = sum_k weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[n, c_in, stride*t + dilation*(k - padding)]` Wait, this is confusing because the convention varies. Let me check: for Conv1d, `weight` is `(out_channels, in_channels // groups, kernel_size)`. But for ConvTranspose1d, `weight` is `(in_channels, out_channels // groups, kernel_size)`. The transpose relationship: If `y = conv1d(x, w, stride=s, padding=p)` then `x ≈ conv_transpose1d(y, w, stride=s, padding=p)` with appropriately sized signals. For Conv1d: `y[t] = sum_k w[k] * x[stride*t + k - padding]` For ConvTranspose1d: `x[t] = sum_k w[k] * y[stride*t - k + padding]`... roughly. Let me think more carefully. Consider conv1d with stride=1, padding=0: `y[t] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w[k] * x[t + k]` for valid region. For conv_transpose1d with stride=1, padding=0 (applied to y to get x): `x[t] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w[k] * y[t - k]` for valid region where `t-k` is in range. This means: `x[t] = sum_{k} w[k] * y[t - k]`. Wait, let's double-check with a small example. Say x = [x0, x1, x2, x3], w = [w0, w1], K=2. Conv1d with stride=1, padding=0: y = [w0*x0 + w1*x1, w0*x1 + w1*x2, w0*x2 + w1*x3] Length(y) = 3. Now conv_transpose1d on y with same w, stride=1, padding=0 should give something like x back (at least for causal parts). For length 3 input and K=2, output length = 3 + 2 - 1 = 4. out[0] = sum_k w[k] * y[0 - k] = w[0] * y[0] = w0 * (w0*x0 + w1*x1) -- this doesn't give x0 directly. Actually, the exact formulas for ConvTranspose1d are more subtle. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose1d is implemented by basically reshaping, inserting zeros, and convolving. For stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0: It's essentially a regular convolution with the weight flipped (reflected). Actually, looking at the PyTorch source: ConvTranspose1d for stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to: ``` out = F.conv1d(input, weight.flip(-1), padding=K-1) ``` Wait, no. Let me think about this with a very small example. For Conv1d: `y[i] = sum_{j} w[j] * x[i + j]` for valid region with padding=0. For ConvTranspose1d to be the adjoint: `<Conv1d(x), y> = <x, ConvTranspose(y)>`. `<Conv1d(x), y> = sum_i sum_j w[j] x[i+j] y[i] = sum_t x[t] sum_j w[j] y[t-j]`. So ConvTranspose(y)[t] = sum_j w[j] y[t-j]. For t=0: sum_j w[j] y[-j]. Only j=0 contributes: w[0] * y[0]. For t=1: w[0]*y[1] + w[1]*y[0]. For t=2: w[0]*y[2] + w[1]*y[1] + w[2]*y[0]. ... This is convolution/correlation depending on conventions. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d is NOT the strict adjoint - it includes additional output adjustments. For stride=1, padding=0, dil=1: ``` out[t] = sum_k weight[k] * input[t - k] ``` Wait, I need to just verify by writing a small test. But looking at my kernel: I used: ``` out[tx * 2 + j] = sum_{ic} sum_k input[tx * 2 + (K-1) - k + j] * weight[k] ``` For K=3: out[0] = sum_k input[2 - k] * w[k] = input[2]*w[0] + input[1]*w[1] + input[0]*w[2] out[1] = sum_k input[3 - k] * w[k] = input[3]*w[0] + input[2]*w[1] + input[1]*w[2] For PyTorch ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0: out[0] = sum_k input[0 - k] * w[k]... this is only input[0] * w[0] (for k=0 and valid index). out[1] = input[1]*w[0] + input[0]*w[1] out[2] = input[2]*w[0] + input[1]*w[1] + input[0]*w[2] Wait, my implementation might actually be computing a regular conv1d with flipped kernel, not a conv transpose! For conv transpose with stride=1, padding=0, the output length should be L + K - 1. With regular conv1d and padding=K-1, length is L + 2*(K-1) - (K-1) + 1 = L + K - 1. So they are the same length! Actually, ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0 is the same as conv1d with weight flipped and padding=K-1!! Let me verify. Conv1d with weight flipped by -1: w'[k] = w[K-1-k]. Conv1d with padding=K-1 on input x: out[t] = sum_k w'[k] * x[t + k - (K-1)] = sum_k w[K-1-k] * x[t + k - (K-1)] Let j = K-1-k, so k = K-1-j: out[t] = sum_j w[j] * x[t - j] This is the adjoint transposed convolution formula I derived above! So ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=0 IS equivalent to Conv1d with flipped weight and padding=K-1. In my kernel I computed: ``` s_in[ic_local * in_len + (tx * 2 + (K - 1) - k)] ``` This means I used `input[tx * 2 + (K - 1) - k]` as if it were a flipped correlation. For the ConvTranspose1d formula `out[t] = sum_k w[k] * x[t - k]` with K=3: out[0] = w[0]*x[0] + w[1]*x[-1] + w[2]*x[-2] = w[0]*x[0] (only) out[1] = w[0]*x[1] + w[1]*x[0] + w[2]*x[-1] = w[0]*x[1] + w[1]*x[0] out[2] = w[0]*x[2] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[0] But in my kernel: For tx=0, K=3, t1=0: sum_k input[0 + (3-1) - k] * w[k] = input[2]*w[0] + input[1]*w[1] + input[0]*w[2] For tx=0, t2=1: sum_k input[1 + (3-1) - k] * w[k] = input[3]*w[0] + input[2]*w[1] + input[1]*w[2] For tx=1, t1=2: sum_k input[2 + (3-1) - k] * w[k] = input[4]*w[0] + input[3]*w[1] + input[2]*w[2] This is giving `sum_k input[t + K - 1 - k] * w[k]`. This is equivalent to `sum_j input[j] * w[j - t + K - 1]`. This doesn't look like the conv transpose formula. Actually, for conv1d with flipped weight and padding=K-1: `out[t] = sum_k w'[k] * x[t + k - (K-1)]` where w'[k] = w[K-1-k]. = `sum_k w[K-1-k] * x[t + k - (K-1)]` Let j = K-1-k: = `sum_j w[j] * x[t - j]` For t=0: w[0]*x[0] + w[1]*x[-1] + w[2]*x[-2] = w[0]*x[0] For t=1: w[0]*x[1] + w[1]*x[0] + w[2]*x[-1] = w[0]*x[1] + w[1]*x[0] For t=2: w[0]*x[2] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[0] But my kernel computed: For t=0: out[0] = w[0]*x[2] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[0] This is actually the regular conv1d with padding=0, not flipped! For conv1d with padding=0: `out[t] = sum_k w[k] * x[t + k]` For t=0: w[0]*x[0] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[2] For t=1: w[0]*x[1] + w[1]*x[2] + w[2]*x[3] Hmm, my output for t=0 is w[0]*x[2] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[0]. This is like `sum_k w[k] * x[K-1-k]`, which is conv1d with fully padded left side. Wait: for conv1d with padding=K-1, x_padded = [0,0, x[0], x[1], x[2], ...] out[0] = sum_k w[k] * x_padded[0+k] = w[0]*x_padded[0] + w[1]*x_padded[1] + w[2]*x_padded[2] = w[0]*0 + w[1]*0 + w[2]*x[0] out[1] = w[0]*0 + w[1]*x[0] + w[2]*x[1] out[2] = w[0]*x[0] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[2] But my kernel gives for t=0: w[0]*x[2] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[0]. Actually, my formula is: `input[(K-1) - k]` for output t=0. out[0] = sum_k w[k] * input[(K-1) - k] out[1] = sum_k w[k] * input[K - k] out[t] = sum_k w[k] * input[t + (K-1) - k] This is `out[t] = sum_k w[k] * input[t + K - 1 - k]`. My naive kernel has a bug - using 1 thread per block and launching B*OC*L_out blocks is going to be super slow and maybe cause kernel launch issues. Also I need to understand why my shared memory kernel was wrong. Let me debug the correct algorithm first. For conv_transpose1d with stride=1, padding=0: It's basically a full convolution but with flip. The formula is: output[b, oc, t] = sum_ic sum_k input[b, ic, t - k + padding] * weight[ic, oc, K-1-k] Wait, for transposed convolution with stride=1 and padding=0, the result is: output_len = input_len + kernel_size - 1 And output[t] = sum over ic, k: input[t - k + padding ...] * weight[k] Let me verify: - For output position t=0: only k=0 contributes with input[0] - For output position t=1: k=0 contributes with input[1], k=1 contributes with input[0] - For output position t: k contributes with input[t-k] if 0 <= t-k < L So: output[b, oc, t] = sum_ic sum_{k=0}^{K-1} input[b, ic, t-k] * weight[ic, oc, K-1-k] Wait wait wait, that's assuming PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d with stride=1 and padding=0 is equivalent to convolving with flipped weight. Let me verify by checking PyTorch docs. Actually, for ConvTranspose1d with stride=1 and padding=0: output = input convolved with weight reversed on the spatial dimension (transposed from forward convolution) Let me think about it using the forward conv relationship. In conv_transpose1d with stride=1, if forward conv with weight w gives y[t] = sum_k x[t+k] * w[k], then conv_transpose is to solve x from y. Actually the simplest test is to use PyTorch and compare with manual computation. But wait - my first fast kernel had: ``` float in_val = s_in[ic_local * in_len + (tx * 2 + (K - 1) - k)]; ``` This loads an input value at position (tx*2 + (K-1) - k) in the shared buffer. But the shared buffer starts at t0-(K-1). So the actual input t_global is: t_global = (t0 - (K-1)) + tx*2 + (K-1) - k = t0 + tx*2 - k = t1 - k That's correct because t1 should use input[t1-k]. And the weight uses: weight[(ic_local * OC_TILE + oc_local) * K + k] This uses weight[ic, oc, k] directly. But ConvTranspose1d should use reversed weight? Let me think... reasoning
The naive kernel is correct but very slow. Let me try to optimize it with a shared-memory tiled approach. The key insight for conv_transpose1d with stride=1, padding=0 is: For each output point t, we sum over input channels ic and kernel positions k: out[b, oc, t] = sum_{ic} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} in[b, ic, t-k] * weight[ic, oc, k] This is equivalent to a standard conv1d where the kernel is reversed (or keeping as-is with t-k indexing). For optimization, I'll use a tile-based approach where each thread block computes a tile of outputs. Let me use a more efficient kernel with: - Each thread computes multiple outputs - Shared memory for input tiles - Coalesced memory access Let me write a more optimized kernel but make sure it's correct. The previous issue was likely with shared memory indexing. Let me carefully craft the indexing. For a block computing outputs [t0:t0+T) for a subset of OC channels: - We need inputs [t0-(K-1):t0+T) across IC channels - Each output t requires: sum_{ic} sum_{k} in[b, ic, t-k] * w[ic, oc, k] I need to load input for t_global = t0 - (K-1) to t0 + T - 1, which is T + K - 1 elements per IC. And weight for all IC and OC in the block. For correctness, let me verify with a specific example: - K=3, t0=0, T=4 - We need outputs at t=0,1,2,3 - For t=0: sum_k in[0-k] * w[k] = in[0]*w[0] + in[-1]*w[1] + in[-2]*w[2] = in[0]*w[0] (boundary) - For t=1: in[1]*w[0] + in[0]*w[1] - For t=2: in[2]*w[0] + in[1]*w[1] + in[0]*w[2] In shared memory, if we store in[t_local] where t_local maps to t_global = t0 - (K-1) + t_local: - t_local=0 → t_global=-2 (boundary, 0) - t_local=1 → t_global=-1 (boundary, 0) - t_local=2 → t_global=0, value = in[0] - t_local=3 → t_global=1, value = in[1] - t_local=4 → t_global=2, value = in[2] - t_local=5 → t_global=3, value = in[3] For output t=0 (which is t_local=0 in the tile): We need in[0-k] for k=0,1,2, which are t_global = 0, -1, -2 In shared memory: t_global=0 → t_local=2, t_global=-1 → t_local=1, t_global=-2 → t_local=0 So we access s_in[t_local=2-0=2] for k=0? No wait... Actually, for t_local in [0, T): - Need in[t - k] = in[(t0 + t_local) - k] - In shared memory, this is at position (t0 + t_local - k) - (t0 - (K-1)) = t_local + K - 1 - k So for t_local=0, k=0: shared index = 0 + K - 1 - 0 = K - 1 = 2 ✓ For t_local=0, k=1: shared index = 0 + 2 - 1 = 1 ✓ For t_local=0, k=2: shared index = 0 + 2 - 2 = 0 ✓ For t_local=1, k=0: shared index = 1 + 2 - 0 = 3 ✓ For t_local=1, k=1: shared index = 1 + 2 - 1 = 2 ✓ For t_local=1, k=2: shared index = 1 + 2 - 2 = 1 ✓ Great, formula is: shared_input_index = t_local + (K - 1) - k Let me write the optimized kernel with this correct indexing.
